<div class="center">

<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="25.927891156463" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="Brian_Eno_-_Dune_Prophecy_Theme.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="58695" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (59 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/dc/Brian_Eno_-_Dune_Prophecy_Theme.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>

<p><b>Brian Peter George St John le Baptiste de la Salle Eno</b>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Designers_for_Industry" title="Royal Designers for Industry">RDI</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/iː/ long 'e' in 'seed'">iː</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; born 15 May 1948 and originally christened <b>Brian Peter George Eno</b>) is an English musician, composer, record producer, singer, writer, and visual artist. He is best known for his pioneering work in <a href="/wiki/Ambient_music" title="Ambient music">ambient</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic music</a> as well as his contributions to <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Worldbeat" title="Worldbeat">worldbeat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chance_music" title="Chance music">chance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Generative_music" title="Generative music">generative music</a> styles. A self-described "non-musician", Eno has advocated a methodology of "theory over practice" throughout his career, and has helped to introduce a variety of unique recording techniques and conceptual approaches into contemporary music. He has been described as one of <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">popular music</a>'s most influential and innovative figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ankeny_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-ankeny-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-rs-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nerve_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-nerve-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Suffolk" title="Suffolk">Suffolk</a>, Eno studied <a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">painting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Experimental_music" title="Experimental music">experimental music</a> at <a href="/wiki/Art_school" title="Art school">art school</a> in the late 1960s before joining <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">glam rock</a> group <a href="/wiki/Roxy_Music" title="Roxy Music">Roxy Music</a> as <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizer</a> player in 1971. After recording two albums with the band, he departed in 1973 to record a number of solo albums, ultimately helping to develop ambient music with works such as <i><a href="/wiki/Another_Green_World" title="Another Green World">Another Green World</a></i> (1975), <i><a href="/wiki/Discreet_Music" title="Discreet Music">Discreet Music</a></i> (1975), and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_for_Airports" title="Music for Airports">Music for Airports</a></i> (1978). He took part in frequent collaborations with artists such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fripp" title="Robert Fripp">Robert Fripp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cluster_(band)" title="Cluster (band)">Cluster</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a> on his "<a href="/wiki/Berlin_Trilogy" title="Berlin Trilogy">Berlin Trilogy</a>", and <a href="/wiki/David_Byrne" title="David Byrne">David Byrne</a> on 1981's <i><a href="/wiki/My_Life_in_the_Bush_of_Ghosts_(album)" title="My Life in the Bush of Ghosts (album)">My Life in the Bush of Ghosts</a></i>. During the 1970s, Eno would also begin a parallel career as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_producer" title="Music producer">producer</a>, which included work on albums by <a href="/wiki/Talking_Heads" title="Talking Heads">Talking Heads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Devo" title="Devo">Devo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/No_wave" title="No wave">no wave</a> compilation <i><a href="/wiki/No_New_York" title="No New York">No New York</a></i> (1978), and works by <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> artists such as <a href="/wiki/John_Cale" title="John Cale">John Cale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Hassell" title="Jon Hassell">Jon Hassell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laraaji" title="Laraaji">Laraaji</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Budd" title="Harold Budd">Harold Budd</a>, among others.</p>
<p>In subsequent decades, Eno continued to record solo albums, collaborate, and produce for other artists, including <a href="/wiki/U2" title="U2">U2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coldplay" title="Coldplay">Coldplay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laurie_Anderson" title="Laurie Anderson">Laurie Anderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_(band)" title="James (band)">James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grace_Jones" title="Grace Jones">Grace Jones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slowdive" title="Slowdive">Slowdive</a>. Dating back to his time as a student, he has also pursued a variety of multimedia projects in parallel with his music career, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_installations" title="Sound installations">sound installations</a> and his mid-70s co-development of <a href="/wiki/Oblique_Strategies" title="Oblique Strategies">Oblique Strategies</a>, a deck of cards featuring cryptic aphorisms intended to break <a href="/wiki/Writer%27s_block" title="Writer's block">creative blocks</a> and encourage <a href="/wiki/Lateral_thinking" title="Lateral thinking">lateral thinking</a>. He continues to release music, produce, and write, and maintains a regular column in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prospect_Magazine" title="Prospect Magazine">Prospect Magazine</a></i>.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Eno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Brian Eno was born in 1948 at Phyllis Memorial Hospital, <a href="/wiki/Woodbridge,_Suffolk" title="Woodbridge, Suffolk">Woodbridge, Suffolk</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholic</a> parents William Arnold Eno (1916-1988),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> who had followed his father and grandfather into the postal service, and Maria Alphonsine Eno (<i>née</i> Buslot, 1922-2005),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> a woman originally from Belgium who William had met during his service in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The unusual surname Eno, long established in Suffolk, derives from the French <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> surname Hennot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Maria had already had a daughter (Brian's half-sister Rita), and together William and Maria would have two further children, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Eno" title="Roger Eno">Roger</a> (b. 1959) and Arlette (b. 1961).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eno was educated at <a href="/wiki/St_Joseph%27s_College,_Ipswich" title="St Joseph's College, Ipswich">St Joseph's College, Ipswich</a>, which was founded by the <a href="/wiki/De_La_Salle_Brothers" title="De La Salle Brothers">De La Salle Brothers</a> order of Catholic brothers (from whom he took part of his name when a student there),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> at Ipswich Art School in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cybernetic" title="Cybernetic">cybernetic</a> theorist <a href="/wiki/Roy_Ascott" title="Roy Ascott">Roy Ascott</a>'s Groundcourse and the <a href="/wiki/Winchester_School_of_Art" title="Winchester School of Art">Winchester School of Art</a>, graduating in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> At the Winchester School of Art, Eno attended a lecture by <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">the Who</a> about the use of tape machines by non-musicians, citing the lecture as the moment he realised he could make music even though he was not a musician at that point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>In school, Eno used a <a href="/wiki/Tape_recorder" title="Tape recorder">tape recorder</a> as a musical instrument and experimented with his first, sometimes <a href="/wiki/Improvisation" title="Improvisation">improvisational</a>, bands. St. Joseph's College teacher and painter <a href="/wiki/Tom_Phillips_(artist)" title="Tom Phillips (artist)">Tom Phillips</a> encouraged him, recalling "Piano Tennis" with Eno, in which, after collecting pianos, they stripped and aligned them in a hall, striking them with tennis balls. From that collaboration, he became involved in <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_Cardew" title="Cornelius Cardew">Cornelius Cardew</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Scratch_Orchestra" title="Scratch Orchestra">Scratch Orchestra</a>. The first released recording in which Eno played is the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Grammophon" title="Deutsche Grammophon">Deutsche Grammophon</a> edition of Cardew's <i>The Great Learning</i> (recorded February 1971), as one of the voices in the recital of Paragraph 7 of <i>The Great Learning</i>. Another early recording was the <i>Berlin Horse</i> soundtrack, by Malcom Le Grice, a nine-minute, 2 × 16 mm-double-projection, released in 1970 and presented in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Eno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Eno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roxy_Music" title="Roxy Music">Roxy Music</a></div>
<p>Eno's professional music career began in London, as a member (1971–1973) of the <a href="/wiki/Glam_rock" title="Glam rock">glam</a>/<a href="/wiki/Art_rock" title="Art rock">art rock</a> band Roxy Music, initially not appearing on stage with them at live shows, but operating the <a href="/wiki/Mixing_console" title="Mixing console">mixing desk</a>, processing the band's sound with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VCS3" title="VCS3">VCS3</a> synthesiser and tape recorders, and singing <a href="/wiki/Backing_vocalist" title="Backing vocalist">backing vocals</a>. He then progressed to appearing on stage as a performing member of the group, usually flamboyantly costumed. He quit the band on completing the promotional tour for the band's second album, <i><a href="/wiki/For_Your_Pleasure" title="For Your Pleasure">For Your Pleasure</a>,</i> because of disagreements with lead singer <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Ferry" title="Bryan Ferry">Bryan Ferry</a> and boredom with the rock star life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1992, he described his Roxy Music tenure as important to his career: "As a result of going into a subway station and meeting [saxophonist <a href="/wiki/Andy_Mackay" title="Andy Mackay">Andy Mackay</a>], I joined Roxy Music, and, as a result of that, I have a career in music. If I'd walked ten yards further on the platform, or missed that train, or been in the next carriage, I probably would have been an art teacher now".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prendergast_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prendergast-14">[14]</a></sup> During his period with Roxy Music, and for his first three solo albums, he was credited on records only as 'Eno'.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="503" data-file-width="500" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png/230px-Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png/345px-Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png/460px-Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png 2x" width="230"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brian_Eno_-_TopPop_1974_12.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Eno acting in Dutch television (1974)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eno embarked on a solo career almost immediately. Between 1973 and 1977 he created four albums of electronically inflected <a href="/wiki/Art_pop" title="Art pop">art pop</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Here_Come_the_Warm_Jets" title="Here Come the Warm Jets">Here Come the Warm Jets</a></i> (1973), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taking_Tiger_Mountain_By_Strategy_(album)" title="Taking Tiger Mountain By Strategy (album)">Taking Tiger Mountain (By Strategy)</a></i> (1974), <i><a href="/wiki/Another_Green_World" title="Another Green World">Another Green World</a></i> (1975), and <i><a href="/wiki/Before_and_After_Science" title="Before and After Science">Before and After Science</a></i> (1977). <i>Tiger Mountain</i> contains the galloping "Third Uncle", one of Eno's best-known songs, owing in part to its later being covered by <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus_(band)" title="Bauhaus (band)">Bauhaus</a> and <a href="/wiki/801_(band)" title="801 (band)">801</a>. Critic <a href="/wiki/Dave_Thompson_(author)" title="Dave Thompson (author)">Dave Thompson</a> writes that the song is "a near punk attack of riffing guitars and clattering percussion, 'Third Uncle' could, in other hands, be a heavy metal anthem, albeit one whose lyrical content would tongue-tie the most slavish air guitarist."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>These four albums were remastered and reissued in 2004 by <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Records" title="Virgin Records">Virgin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Astralwerks" title="Astralwerks">Astralwerks</a> label. Due to Eno's decision not to add any extra tracks of the original material, a handful of tracks originally issued as singles have not been reissued ("Seven Deadly Finns" and "The Lion Sleeps Tonight" were included on the <a href="/wiki/Deletion_(music_industry)" title="Deletion (music industry)">deleted</a> <i>Eno Box II: Vocal</i> and the single mix of "King's Lead Hat", the title of which is an <a href="/wiki/Anagram" title="Anagram">anagram</a> of "<a href="/wiki/Talking_Heads" title="Talking Heads">Talking Heads</a>", has never been reissued).</p>
<p>During this period, Eno also played three dates with <a href="/wiki/Phil_Manzanera" title="Phil Manzanera">Phil Manzanera</a> in the band <a href="/wiki/801_(band)" title="801 (band)">801</a>, a "<a href="/wiki/Supergroup_(music)" title="Supergroup (music)">supergroup</a>" that performed more or less mutated selections from albums by Eno, Manzanera, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quiet_Sun_(band)" title="Quiet Sun (band)">Quiet Sun</a>, as well as covers of songs by <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> ("<a href="/wiki/Tomorrow_Never_Knows" title="Tomorrow Never Knows">Tomorrow Never Knows</a>") and <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">The Kinks</a> ("<a href="/wiki/You_Really_Got_Me" title="You Really Got Me">You Really Got Me</a>").</p>
<p>In 1972, Eno and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fripp" title="Robert Fripp">Robert Fripp</a> (from <a href="/wiki/King_Crimson" title="King Crimson">King Crimson</a>) developed the "<a href="/wiki/Frippertronics" title="Frippertronics">Frippertronics</a>" <a href="/wiki/Delay_(audio_effect)" title="Delay (audio effect)">tape delay</a> system; a year later, the pair released the proto-ambient album <i><a href="/wiki/(No_Pussyfooting)" title="(No Pussyfooting)">(No Pussyfooting)</a></i> (1973).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBCEquatorialStars_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBCEquatorialStars-17">[17]</a></sup> The technique involved two <a href="/wiki/Revox" title="Revox">Revox</a> tape recorders set up side by side, with the tape unspooling from the first deck being carried over to the second deck to be spooled. This enabled sound recorded on the first deck to be played back by the second deck at a time delay that varied with the distance between the two decks and the speed of the tape (typically a few seconds). The technique was borrowed from minimalist composer <a href="/wiki/Terry_Riley" title="Terry Riley">Terry Riley</a>, whose similar tape-delay feedback system with a pair of Revox tape recorders (a setup Riley used to call the "Time Lag Accumulator") was first used on Riley's album <i>Music for The Gift</i> (1963).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1975, Fripp and Eno released a second album, <i><a href="/wiki/Evening_Star_(album)" title="Evening Star (album)">Evening Star</a></i>, and played several live shows in Europe.</p>
<p>Eno was a prominent member of the performance art-classical orchestra the <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth_Sinfonia" title="Portsmouth Sinfonia">Portsmouth Sinfonia</a> – having started playing with them in 1972. In 1973 he produced the orchestra's first album <i>The Portsmouth Sinfonia Plays the Popular Classics</i> (released in March 1974) and in 1974 he produced the live album <i>Hallellujah! The Portsmouth Sinfonia Live at the Royal Albert Hall</i> of their infamous May 1974 concert (released in October 1974). In addition to producing both albums, Eno performed in the orchestra on both recordings – playing the clarinet. Eno also deployed the orchestra's famously dissonant string section on his second solo album <i>Taking Tiger Mountain (By Strategy)</i>. The orchestra at this time included other musicians whose solo work he would subsequently release on his Obscure label including <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Bryars" title="Gavin Bryars">Gavin Bryars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Nyman" title="Michael Nyman">Michael Nyman</a>. That year he also composed music for the album <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_June%27s_Linguistic_Leprosy" title="Lady June's Linguistic Leprosy">Lady June's Linguistic Leprosy</a></i>, with <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Ayers" title="Kevin Ayers">Kevin Ayers</a>, to accompany the poet <a href="/wiki/Lady_June" title="Lady June">June Campbell Cramer</a>.</p>
<p>Eno continued his career by producing a larger number of highly eclectic and increasingly <a href="/wiki/Ambient_music" title="Ambient music">ambient</a> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_music" title="Electronic music">electronic</a> and acoustic albums. He is widely credited with coining the term "ambient music",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> low-volume music designed to modify one's perception of a surrounding environment.</p>
<p>His first such work, 1975's <i><a href="/wiki/Discreet_Music" title="Discreet Music">Discreet Music</a></i> (again created via an elaborate tape-delay methodology, which Eno diagrammed on the back cover of the LP), is considered the landmark album of the genre. This was followed by his <i>Ambient</i> series (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Music_for_Airports" title="Music for Airports">Music for Airports</a> (Ambient 1)</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Plateaux_of_Mirror" title="The Plateaux of Mirror">The Plateaux of Mirror</a> (Ambient 2)</i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Radiance" title="Day of Radiance">Day of Radiance</a> (Ambient 3)</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_Land" title="On Land">On Land</a> (Ambient 4)</i>). Eno was the primary musician on these releases with the exception of <i>Ambient 2</i> which featured <a href="/wiki/Harold_Budd" title="Harold Budd">Harold Budd</a> on keyboard, and <i>Ambient 3</i> where the American composer <a href="/wiki/Laraaji" title="Laraaji">Laraaji</a> was the sole musician playing the <a href="/wiki/Zither" title="Zither">zither</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hammered_dulcimer" title="Hammered dulcimer">hammered dulcimer</a> with Eno producing.</p>
<p>In 1975 Eno performed as the Wolf in a rock version of <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Prokofiev" title="Sergei Prokofiev">Sergei Prokofiev</a>'s classic <i><a href="/wiki/Peter_and_the_Wolf" title="Peter and the Wolf">Peter and the Wolf</a></i>. Produced by <a href="/wiki/Robin_Lumley" title="Robin Lumley">Robin Lumley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Lancaster" title="Jack Lancaster">Jack Lancaster</a>, the album featured <a href="/wiki/Gary_Moore" title="Gary Moore">Gary Moore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manfred_Mann" title="Manfred Mann">Manfred Mann</a>, Phil Collins, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stephane_Grapelli" title="Stephane Grapelli">Stephane Grapelli</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chris_Spedding" title="Chris Spedding">Chris Spedding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cozy_Powell" title="Cozy Powell">Cozy Powell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jon_Hiseman" title="Jon Hiseman">Jon Hiseman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Bruford" title="Bill Bruford">Bill Bruford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Lee" title="Alvin Lee">Alvin Lee</a>. Also in 1975, Eno provided synthesisers and treatments on <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Sun" title="Quiet Sun">Quiet Sun</a>'s <i>Mainstream</i> album alongside Phil Manzanera, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Hayward_(musician)" title="Charles Hayward (musician)">Charles Hayward</a>, Dave Jarrett, and <a href="/wiki/Bill_MacCormick" title="Bill MacCormick">Bill MacCormick</a>, and he performed on and contributed songs and vocals to Manzanera's <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_Head_(album)" title="Diamond Head (album)">Diamond Head</a></i> album.</p>
<p>In September 1976 Eno recorded with the <a href="/wiki/Krautrock" title="Krautrock">Krautrock/Kosmische Musik</a> group <a href="/wiki/Harmonia_(band)" title="Harmonia (band)">Harmonia</a> at their studio in <a href="/wiki/Forst,_Lower_Saxony" title="Forst, Lower Saxony">Forst</a>, Germany. This material was not released until 1997 as <i><a href="/wiki/Tracks_and_Traces" title="Tracks and Traces">Tracks and Traces</a></i> by Harmonia '76. It was again reissued in 2009 with additional tracks and credited to Harmonia &amp; Eno '76.</p>
<p>From 1976 to 1978 Eno collaborated with David Bowie on Bowie's "Berlin Trilogy" (<i><a href="/wiki/Low_(David_Bowie_album)" title="Low (David Bowie album)">Low</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/%22Heroes%22_(David_Bowie_album)" title='"Heroes" (David Bowie album)'>"Heroes"</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Lodger_(album)" title="Lodger (album)">Lodger</a></i>), and from 1978 to 1980 with Talking Heads (<i><a href="/wiki/More_Songs_About_Buildings_and_Food" title="More Songs About Buildings and Food">More Songs About Buildings and Food</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Fear_of_Music" title="Fear of Music">Fear of Music</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Remain_in_Light" title="Remain in Light">Remain in Light</a></i>).</p>
<p>Eno considered joining XTC in 1980.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Eno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1980 Eno provided a film score for <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Vesely" title="Herbert Vesely">Herbert Vesely</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Egon_Schiele_%E2%80%93_Exzess_und_Bestrafung" title="Egon Schiele – Exzess und Bestrafung">Egon Schiele – Exzess und Bestrafung</a></i>, also known as <i>Egon Schiele – Excess and Punishment</i>. The ambient-style score was an unusual choice for a historical piece, but it worked effectively with the film's themes of sexual obsession and death.</p>
<p>In 1981, after he returned from <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and before making <i>On Land,</i> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Quine" title="Robert Quine">Robert Quine</a> played him <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles Davis</a>' 1974 track "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Get_Up_With_It" title="Get Up With It">He Loved Him Madly</a>", a melancholy tribute to <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a> influenced by both <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Africa" title="Music of Africa">African music</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karlheinz_Stockhausen" title="Karlheinz Stockhausen">Karlheinz Stockhausen</a>: as Eno stated in the liner notes for <i>On Land</i>, "Teo Macero's revolutionary production on that piece seemed to me to have the "spacious" quality I was after, and like Federico Fellini's 1973 film <i>Amarcord</i>, it too became a touchstone to which I returned frequently."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1980–1981 Eno collaborated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Byrne_(musician)" title="David Byrne (musician)">David Byrne</a> of <a href="/wiki/Talking_Heads" title="Talking Heads">Talking Heads</a> on <a href="/wiki/My_Life_in_the_Bush_of_Ghosts_(album)" title="My Life in the Bush of Ghosts (album)"><i>My Life in the Bush of Ghosts</i></a>, which was built around radio broadcasts Eno collected while living in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Sampling_(music)" title="Sampling (music)">sampling</a> recordings from around the world transposed over music predominantly inspired by African and <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle Eastern</a> rhythms.</p>
<p>In 1983 Eno collaborated with his brother, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Eno" title="Roger Eno">Roger Eno</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Lanois" title="Daniel Lanois">Daniel Lanois</a> on the album <i><a href="/wiki/Apollo:_Atmospheres_and_Soundtracks" title="Apollo: Atmospheres and Soundtracks">Apollo: Atmospheres and Soundtracks</a></i>. Many of the sounds created on this album can be heard again on later albums produced by both Eno and Lanois. Tracks from the album are also used as part of the musical score for the Al Reinert film, <i><a href="/wiki/For_All_Mankind" title="For All Mankind">For All Mankind</a></i> (1989).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Brian_Eno&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-BLP_unsourced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section of a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Biographies_of_living_persons" title="Wikipedia:Biographies of living persons">biography of a living person</a> <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">include</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">references or sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help by adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">reliable sources</a>.
